<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">God</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the belief of a supreme being in monotheistic thought. For the general faith in a supreme being, see <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">Deity</a>. For God in specific religions, see <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">Conceptions of God</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/God_(disambiguation)" title="God (disambiguation)">God (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Principal object of faith in monotheism</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti href="/wiki/Monad_(philosophy)" title="Monad (philosophy)">Monad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balinese_Hinduism" title="Balinese Hinduism">Balinese Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> thought, <b>God</b> is usually viewed as the supreme being, <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator</a>, and principal object of <a href="/wiki/Faith#Religious_views" title="Faith">faith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">[1]</a></sup> God is usually <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">conceived</a> of as being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnipotent" title="Omnipotent">omnipotent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omniscient" title="Omniscient">omniscient</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnipresent" title="Omnipresent">omnipresent</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnibenevolent" title="Omnibenevolent">omnibenevolent</a>, as well as having an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eternal_existence" title="Eternal existence">eternal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_necessity" title="Metaphysical necessity">necessary existence</a>. God is most often held to be <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">incorporeal</a>, with said characteristic being related to conceptions of <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some religions describe God without reference <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">to gender</a>, while others use terminology that is gender-specific and <span class="nowrap">gender-biased</span>. God has been conceived as either <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal</a> or impersonal. In <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>, God is the creator and <a href="/wiki/God_the_Sustainer" title="God the Sustainer">sustainer</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, while in <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>, God is the creator, but not the sustainer, of the universe. In <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, God is the universe itself, while in <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">panentheism</a>, the universe is part (but not the whole) of God. <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">Atheism</a> is an absence of belief in any God or <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a> is the belief that the existence of God unknown or unknowable. God has also been conceived as the source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">moral obligation</a>, and the "greatest conceivable existent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many notable theologians and philosophers have developed arguments for and against the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platinga-4">[4]</a></sup> God is referred to by different <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">names</a> depending on the language and cultural tradition with titles sometimes used referring to God's attributes.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">Etymology and usage</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/God_(word)" title="God (word)"><i>God</i> (word)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mesha_Stele_(511142469)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Mesha_Stele_%28511142469%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mesha_Stele_(511142469)_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">Mesha Stele</a> bears the earliest known reference (840 BCE) to the Israelite God Yahweh.</div></div></div>
<p>
The earliest written form of the Germanic word <i>God</i> comes from the 6th-century <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">Codex Argenteus</a></i>. The English word itself is derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> * ǥuđan. The reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> form <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">* ǵhu-tó-m</span></span> was likely based on the root <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">* ǵhau(ə)-</span></span>, which meant either "to call" or "to invoke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Germanic words for <i>God</i> were originally <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">neuter</a>—applying to both genders—but during the process of the <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianization</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic peoples</a> from their indigenous <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>, the words became a <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">masculine syntactic form</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BARNHART323_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BARNHART323-6">[6]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, capitalization is used when the word is used as a <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun">proper noun</a>, as well as for other names by which a god is known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Consequently, the capitalized form of <i>god</i> is not used for multiple gods or when used to refer to the generic idea of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
The English word <i>God</i> and its counterparts in other languages are normally used for any and all conceptions and, in spite of significant differences between religions, the term remains an English translation common to all.
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_(god)" title="El (god)">El</a></i> is <i>God</i> in Hebrew, but <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">in Judaism</a>, God is also given a proper name, the <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">tetragrammaton</a> YHWH, in origin possibly the name of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edomite" title="Edomite">Edomite</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midianite" title="Midianite">Midianite</a> deity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahweh_(Canaanite_deity)" title="Yahweh (Canaanite deity)">Yahweh</a>. In many English translations of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, when the word <i>LORD</i> is in all capitals, it signifies that the word represents the tetragrammaton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton2006-10">[10]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> some of the Hebrew titles of God are considered <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">holy names</a>.
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All%C4%81h" title="Allāh">Allāh</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الله</span>) is the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> term with no <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> used by Muslims and Arabic speaking Christians and Jews meaning "The God", while <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BEil%C4%81h" title="ʾilāh">ʾilāh</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">إِلَٰه</span> plural <i>`āliha</i>  آلِهَة) is the term used for a deity or a god in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> also use a <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">multitude of other titles</a> for God.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> is often considered a <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> concept of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> God may also be given a proper name in monotheistic currents of Hinduism which emphasize the <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal nature of God</a>, with early references to his name as <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>-<a href="/wiki/Vasudeva" title="Vasudeva">Vasudeva</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata" title="Bhagavata">Bhagavata</a> or later <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hari" title="Hari">Hari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hastings541_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings541-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sang_Hyang_Widhi_Wasa" title="Sang Hyang Widhi Wasa">Sang Hyang Widhi Wasa</a> is the term used in <a href="/wiki/Balinese_Hinduism" title="Balinese Hinduism">Balinese Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shangdi" title="Shangdi">Shangdi</a> is conceived as the <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitor</a> (first ancestor) of the universe, intrinsic to it and constantly bringing <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">order</a> to it.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> is the name for God used in <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>. "Mazda", or rather the Avestan stem-form <i>Mazdā-</i>, nominative <i>Mazdå</i>, reflects Proto-Iranian <i>*Mazdāh (female)</i>. It is generally taken to be the proper name of the spirit, and like its <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> cognate <i>medhā</i>, means "intelligence" or "wisdom". Both the Avestan and Sanskrit words reflect <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> <i>*mazdhā-</i>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> mn̩sdʰeh<sub>1</sub>, literally meaning "placing (<i>dʰeh<sub>1</sub></i>) one's mind (<i>*mn̩-s</i>)", hence "wise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983685_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983685-17">[17]</a></sup> Meanwhile <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Zoroastrianism" title="Names of God in Zoroastrianism">101 other names</a> are also in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">vāhigurū</i></span></span></i>) is a term most often used in <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> to refer to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> It means "Wonderful Teacher" in the Punjabi language. <i>Vāhi</i> (a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> borrowing) means "wonderful" and <i><a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a></i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">guru</i></span></span></i>) is a term denoting "teacher". Waheguru is also described by some as an experience of ecstasy which is beyond all descriptions. The most common usage of the word "Waheguru" is in the greeting Sikhs use with each other - <i>Waheguru Ji Ka Khalsa, Waheguru Ji Ki Fateh</i> "Wonderful Lord's <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, Victory is to the Wonderful Lord."
</p><p><i>Baha</i>, the "greatest" name for God in the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>, is Arabic for "All-Glorious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other names for God include <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> in ancient Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Atenism</a> where Aten was proclaimed to be the one "true" Supreme Being and creator of the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chukwu" title="Chukwu">Chukwu</a> in <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hayyi_Rabbi" title="Hayyi Rabbi">Hayyi Rabbi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckley_2002_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley_2002-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nashmi_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nashmi-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_conceptions">General conceptions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">Conceptions of God</a></div>
<p>The philosophy of religion recognizes the following as essential attributes of God:
</p>
<ul><li>Omnipotence (limitless power)</li>
<li>Omniscience (limitless knowledge)</li>
<li>Eternity (God is not bound by time)</li>
<li>Goodness (God is wholly benevolent)</li>
<li>Unity (God cannot be divided)</li>
<li>Simplicity (God is not composite)</li>
<li>Incorporeality (God is not material)</li>
<li>Immutability (God is not subject to change)</li>
<li>Impassability (God is not affected)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunninYu2008188_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunninYu2008188-26">[26]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There is no clear consensus on the nature or the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were also various conceptions of God in the ancient <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_theology" title="Aristotelian theology">Aristotle's view of an unmoved mover</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonic concept of the One</a> and the pantheistic God of <a href="/wiki/Stoic_physics" title="Stoic physics">Stoic Physics</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">Theism</a> generally holds that God exists objectively and independently of human thought; that God created and sustains everything; that God is omnipotent and eternal; and that God is personal and interacting with the universe through, for example, <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">religious experience</a> and the prayers of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-smart-28">[28]</a></sup> Theism holds that God is both transcendent and immanent; thus, God is simultaneously <a href="/wiki/Infinity_(philosophy)" title="Infinity (philosophy)">infinite</a> and, in some way, present in the affairs of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-lemos-29">[29]</a></sup> Not all theists subscribe to all of these propositions, but each usually subscribes to some of them (see, by way of comparison, <a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">family resemblance</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-smart-28">[28]</a></sup> Catholic theology holds that God is infinitely <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">simple</a> and is not involuntarily subject to time. Some theists ascribe to God a self-conscious or purposeful limiting of omnipotence, omniscience, or benevolence. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Theism" title="Open Theism">Open Theism</a>, by contrast, contends that, due to the nature of time, God's omniscience does not mean the deity can predict the future. <i>Theism</i> is sometimes used to refer in general to any belief in a god or gods, i.e., monotheism or <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philosofrelGlossthe_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-philosofrelGlossthe-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFDtheism_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-TFDtheism-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oneness">Oneness</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">Monotheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">Henotheism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/170px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/255px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/340px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trinitarians believe that God is composed of the Father, the Son, and the Holy Spirit.</div></div></div>
<p>Monotheists believe that there is only one god, and may also believe this god is worshipped in different religions under different names. The view that all theists actually worship the same god, whether they know it or not, is especially emphasized in the Baháʼí Faith, Hinduism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">Idolatry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">equating others</a> to God in some way, is often strongly condemned in monotheistic traditions. 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">Judaism</a> includes some of the oldest monotheistic traditions in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Islam's most fundamental concept is <i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">tawhid</a></i> meaning "oneness" or "uniqueness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_pillars_of_Islam" title="Five pillars of Islam">pillar of Islam</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">oath</a> that forms the basis of the religion and which non-Muslims wishing to convert must recite, declares that "I testify that there is no <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> except God and I testify that Muhammad is the messenger of God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Christianity, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Trinity" title="Doctrine of the Trinity">doctrine of the Trinity</a> describes God as one God in three divine Persons (each of the three Persons is God himself). The Most Holy Trinity comprises<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">God the Son</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">God the Holy Spirit</a>. In the past centuries, this fundamental mystery of the Christian faith was also summarized by the Latin formula <i>Sancta Trinitas, Unus Deus</i> (Holy Trinity, Unique God), reported in the <i><a href="/wiki/Litany_of_the_Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Litany of the Blessed Virgin Mary">Litanias Lauretanas</a></i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/God_in_Hinduism" title="God in Hinduism">God in Hinduism</a> is viewed differently by diverse strands of the religion with most Hindus having faith in a Supreme being who can be manifested in numerous chosen deities. Thus, the religion is sometimes characterized as <i>Polymorphic Monotheism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">Henotheism</a> is the belief and worship of a single god while accepting the existence or possible existence of other deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcendence">Transcendence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">Transcendence</a> is the aspect of God's nature that is completely independent of the material universe and its physical laws. <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a> holds that God is the universe and the universe is God and denies that God transcends the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a> holds that God contains, but is not identical to, the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It is also the view of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Catholic_Church" title="Liberal Catholic Church">Liberal Catholic Church</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Blavatskian)" title="Theosophy (Blavatskian)">Theosophy</a>; some views of Hinduism except <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, which believes in panentheism; Sikhism; some divisions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopaganism" title="Neopaganism">Neopaganism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, along with many varying denominations and individuals within denominations. <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>, Jewish mysticism, paints a pantheistic/panentheistic view of God—which has wide acceptance in <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">Hasidic Judaism</a>, particularly from their founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israel_ben_Eliezer" title="Israel ben Eliezer">The Baal Shem Tov</a>—but only as an addition to the Jewish view of a personal god, not in the original pantheistic sense that denies or limits persona to God.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_as_creator">Role as creator</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blake_God_Blessing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/170px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/255px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/340px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blake_God_Blessing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>God Blessing the Seventh Day</i>, 1805 watercolor painting by <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a></div></div></div>
<p>Deism holds that God is wholly <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a>: God exists, but does not intervene in the world beyond what was necessary to create it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-lemos-29">[29]</a></sup> In this view, God is not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" title="Anthropomorphic">anthropomorphic</a>, and neither answers prayers nor produces miracles. Common in Deism is a belief that God has no interest in humanity and may not even be aware of humanity. <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">Pandeism</a> combines Deism with Pantheistic beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawe-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Pandeism is proposed to explain as to Deism why God would create a universe and then abandon it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-45">[45]</a></sup> and as to Pantheism, the origin and purpose of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most theists hold that God is omnipotent, omniscient, and benevolent, although this belief raises questions about God's responsibility for evil and suffering in the world. <a href="/wiki/Dystheism" title="Dystheism">Dystheism</a>, which is related to <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">theodicy</a>, is a form of theism which holds that God is either not wholly good or is fully malevolent as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">Śramaṇa</a> religions are generally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-creationist" title="Non-creationist">non-creationist</a>, while also holding that there are divine beings (called <i>Devas</i> in <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Jainism)" title="Deva (Jainism)">Jainism</a>) of limited power and lifespan. <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> has <a href="/wiki/Jainism_and_non-creationism" title="Jainism and non-creationism">generally rejected creationism</a>, holding that soul substances (<a href="/wiki/J%C4%ABva_(Jainism)" title="Jīva (Jainism)">Jīva</a>) are uncreated and that time is beginningless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Depending on one's interpretation and tradition, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> can be conceived as being either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-theistic" title="Non-theistic">non-theistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transtheism" title="Transtheism">trans-theistic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheistic" title="Pantheistic">pantheistic</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Creator_in_Buddhism" title="Creator in Buddhism">Buddhism has generally rejected</a> the specific monotheistic view of a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">Creator God</a>. The Buddha criticizes the theory of creationism in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_Texts" title="Early Buddhist Texts">early Buddhist texts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Also, major Indian Buddhist philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Nagarjuna" title="Nagarjuna">Nagarjuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vasubandhu" title="Vasubandhu">Vasubandhu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">Dharmakirti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghosa</a>, consistently critiqued Creator God views put forth by Hindu thinkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_concepts">Other concepts</span></h3>
<p>In modern times, some more abstract concepts have been developed, such as <a href="/wiki/Process_theology" title="Process theology">process theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Open_theism" title="Open theism">open theism</a>. The contemporaneous French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Michel_Henry" title="Michel Henry">Michel Henry</a> has however proposed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenological_definition_of_God" title="Phenomenological definition of God">phenomenological approach and definition of God</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(religion)" title="Phenomenology (religion)">phenomenological</a> essence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenological_life" title="Phenomenological life">Life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>God has also been conceived as being <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">incorporeal</a> (immaterial), a <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal</a> being, the source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">moral obligation</a>, and the "greatest conceivable existent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">[1]</a></sup> These attributes were all supported to varying degrees by the early Jewish, Christian and Muslim theologian philosophers, including <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-54">[54]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Platinga-4">[4]</a></sup> respectively.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-theistic_views">Non-theistic views</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">Evolutionary origin of religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology_of_religion" title="Evolutionary psychology of religion">Evolutionary psychology of religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_the_creator_of_God" title="Problem of the creator of God">Problem of the creator of God</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">Non-theist</a> views about God also vary. Some non-theists avoid the concept of God, whilst accepting that it is significant to many; other non-theists understand God as a symbol of human values and aspirations. The nineteenth-century English <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">Charles Bradlaugh</a> declared that he refused to say "There is no God", because "the word 'God' is to me a sound conveying no clear or distinct affirmation";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> he said more specifically that he disbelieved in the Christian god. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> proposed an approach dividing the world of philosophy into what he called "<a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">non-overlapping magisteria</a>" (NOMA). In this view, questions of the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, such as those relating to the <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> of God, are <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">non</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> and are the proper domain of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>. The methods of science should then be used to answer any empirical question about the natural world, and theology should be used to answer questions about ultimate meaning and moral value. In this view, the perceived lack of any empirical footprint from the magisterium of the supernatural onto natural events makes science the sole player in the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another view, advanced by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, is that the existence of God is an empirical question, on the grounds that "a universe with a god would be a completely different kind of universe from one without, and it would be a scientific difference."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> argued that the doctrine of a Creator of the Universe was difficult to prove or disprove and that the only conceivable scientific discovery that could disprove the existence of a Creator (not necessarily a God) would be the discovery that the universe is infinitely old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> and co-author <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Mlodinow" title="Leonard Mlodinow">Leonard Mlodinow</a> state in their 2010 book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Grand_Design_(book)" title="The Grand Design (book)">The Grand Design</a></i>, that it is reasonable to ask who or what created the universe, but if the answer is God, then the question has merely been deflected to that of who created God. Both authors claim however, that it is possible to answer these questions purely within the realm of science, and without invoking any divine beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agnosticism_and_atheism">Agnosticism and atheism</span></h3>
<p>Agnosticism is the view that the <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth values</a> of certain claims—especially <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> and religious claims such as <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">whether God</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> exist—are unknown and perhaps unknowable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hepburn_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hepburn-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoweRoutledge_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoweRoutledge-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atheism is, in a broad sense, the rejection of <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> in the existence of <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In a narrower sense, atheism is specifically the position that there are no deities, although it can be defined as a lack of belief in the existence of any deities, rather than a positive belief in the nonexistence of any deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropomorphism">Anthropomorphism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">Anthropomorphism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">Pascal Boyer</a> argues that while there is a wide array of supernatural concepts found around the world, in general, supernatural beings tend to behave much like people. The construction of gods and spirits like persons is one of the best known traits of religion. He cites examples from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, which is, in his opinion, more like a modern <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap opera</a> than other religious systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyer_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-boyer-66">[66]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Castel" title="Bertrand du Castel">Bertrand du Castel</a> and Timothy Jurgensen demonstrate through formalization that Boyer's explanatory model matches physics' <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> in positing not directly observable entities as intermediaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ducasteljurgensen_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ducasteljurgensen-67">[67]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">Anthropologist</a> Stewart Guthrie contends that people project human features onto non-human aspects of the world because it makes those aspects more familiar. <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> also suggested that god concepts are projections of one's father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Likewise, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> was one of the earliest to suggest that gods represent an extension of human social life to include supernatural beings. In line with this reasoning, psychologist Matt Rossano contends that when humans began living in larger groups, they may have created gods as a means of enforcing morality. In small groups, morality can be enforced by social forces such as gossip or reputation. However, it is much harder to enforce morality using social forces in much larger groups. Rossano indicates that by including ever-watchful gods and spirits, humans discovered an effective strategy for restraining selfishness and building more cooperative groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernature_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-supernature-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Existence">Existence</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6000" data-file-width="4004" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/170px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/255px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/340px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> summed up <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">five main arguments</a> as proofs for God's existence. (Painting by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Crivelli" title="Carlo Crivelli">Carlo Crivelli</a>, 1476)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton,_1689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2671" data-file-width="2218" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton,_1689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> saw the existence of a Creator necessary in the movement of astronomical objects. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a>, 1689</div></div></div>
<p>Arguments about the existence of God typically include empirical, deductive, and inductive types. Different views include that: "God does not exist" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_and_strong_atheism" title="Weak and strong atheism">strong atheism</a>); "God almost certainly does not exist" (<i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>); "no one knows whether God exists" (<a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> "God exists, but this cannot be proven or disproven" (<i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>); and that "God exists and this can be proven" (<a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">strong theism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Countless arguments have been proposed to prove the existence of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Some of the most notable arguments are the <a href="/wiki/Five_Ways_(Aquinas)" title="Five Ways (Aquinas)">Five Ways of Aquinas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_desire" title="Argument from desire">argument from desire</a> proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">C.S. Lewis</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological_Argument" title="Ontological Argument">Ontological Argument</a> formulated both by <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">René Descartes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anselm's approach was to define God as, "that than which nothing greater can be conceived". Famed pantheist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> would later carry this idea to its extreme: "By God I understand a being absolutely infinite, i.e., a substance consisting of infinite attributes, of which each one expresses an eternal and infinite essence." For Spinoza, the whole of the natural universe is made of one substance, God, or its equivalent, Nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> His proof for the existence of God was a variation of the Ontological argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">Scientist</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> saw <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">the nontrinitarian God</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heretic_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-heretic-75">[75]</a></sup> as the masterful creator whose existence could not be denied in the face of the grandeur of all creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he rejected polymath <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>' thesis that God would necessarily make a perfect world which requires no intervention from the creator. In Query 31 of the <i>Opticks</i>, Newton simultaneously made an argument from design and for the necessity of intervention:<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> For while comets move in very eccentric orbs in all manner of positions, blind fate could never make all the planets move one and the same way in orbs concentric, some inconsiderable irregularities excepted which may have arisen from the mutual actions of comets and planets on one another, and which will be apt to increase, till this system wants a reformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>
Thomas Aquinas asserted that the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a> is self-evident in itself, but not to us: "Therefore I say that this proposition, "God exists", of itself is self-evident, for the predicate is the same as the subject.... Now because we do not know the essence of God, the proposition is not self-evident to us; but needs to be demonstrated by things that are more known to us, though less known in their nature—namely, by effects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Thomas believed that the existence of God can be demonstrated. Briefly in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i> and more extensively in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>, he considered in great detail five arguments for the existence of God, widely known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">quinque viae</a></i> (Five Ways).<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the original text of the five proofs, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">quinque viae</a></div>
<ol><li>Motion: Some things undoubtedly move, though cannot cause their own motion. Since there can be no infinite chain of causes of motion, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Unmoved_mover" title="Unmoved mover">First Mover</a> not moved by anything else, and this is what everyone understands by God.</li>
<li>Causation: As in the case of motion, nothing can cause itself, and an infinite chain of causation is impossible, so there must be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prima_causa" title="Prima causa">First Cause</a>, called God.</li>
<li>Existence of necessary and the unnecessary: Our experience includes things certainly existing but apparently unnecessary. Not everything can be unnecessary, for then once there was nothing and there would still be nothing. Therefore, we are compelled to suppose something that exists necessarily, having this necessity only from itself; in fact itself the cause for other things to exist.</li>
<li>Gradation: If we can notice a gradation in things in the sense that some things are more hot, good, etc., there must be a superlative that is the truest and noblest thing, and so most fully existing. This then, we call God (Note: Thomas does not ascribe actual qualities to God Himself).</li>
<li>Ordered tendencies of nature: A direction of actions to an end is noticed in all bodies following natural laws. Anything without awareness tends to a goal under the guidance of one who is aware. This we call God (Note that even when we guide objects, in Thomas's view, the source of all our knowledge comes from God as well).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Some theologians, such as the scientist and theologian <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a>, argue that the existence of God is not a question that can be answered using the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath2005_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrath2005-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barackman2001_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-barackman2001-81">[81]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">Agnostic</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> argues that science and religion are not in conflict and do not <a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">overlap</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some findings in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> are interpreted by some atheists (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawrence_M._Krauss" title="Lawrence M. Krauss">Lawrence M. Krauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Harris" title="Sam Harris">Sam Harris</a>) as evidence that God is an imaginary entity only, with no basis in reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris,_S_2005_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris,_S_2005-84">[84]</a></sup> These atheists argue that a single, omniscient God who is imagined to have created the universe and is particularly attentive to the lives of humans has been imagined, embellished and promulgated in a trans-generational manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> interprets such findings not only as a lack of evidence for the material existence of such a God, but as extensive evidence to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> held that the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_morality" title="Argument from morality">argument from morality</a> is valid.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_creation">Relationship with creation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">Creator deity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duerer-Prayer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="842" data-file-width="575" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Duerer-Prayer.jpg/220px-Duerer-Prayer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Duerer-Prayer.jpg/330px-Duerer-Prayer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Duerer-Prayer.jpg/440px-Duerer-Prayer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duerer-Prayer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Praying_Hands_(D%C3%BCrer)" title="Praying Hands (Dürer)">Praying Hands</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship">Worship</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">Worship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">Prayer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Supplication" title="Supplication">Supplication</a></div>
<p>Theistic religious traditions often require <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> of God. Muslims believe that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purpose_of_life" title="Purpose of life">purpose of existence</a> is to <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patheos1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-patheos1-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> To address the issue of an all-powerful being demanding to be worshipped, it is held that God does not need or benefit from worship but that worship is for the benefit of the worshipper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gandhi" title="Gandhi">Gandhi</a> expressed the view that God does not need his supplication and that "Prayer is not an asking. It is a longing of the soul. It is a daily admission of one's weakness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Invoking God in <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a> plays a significant role among many believers. Depending on the tradition, God can be viewed as a personal God who is only to be invoked directly while other traditions allow praying to intermediaries, such as <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Intercession" title="Intercession">intercede</a> on their behalf. Prayer often also includes <a href="/wiki/Supplication" title="Supplication">supplication</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness#Religious_views" title="Forgiveness">asking forgiveness</a>. God is often believed to be forgiving. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> states God would replace a sinless people with one who sinned but still asked repentance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">Sacrifice</a> for the sake of God is another act of devotion that includes <a href="/wiki/Fasting#religious_views" title="Fasting">fasting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almsgiving" title="Almsgiving">almsgiving</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">Remembrance</a> of God in daily life include mentioning interjections <a href="/wiki/Alhamdulillah" title="Alhamdulillah">thanking God</a> when feeling gratitude or <a href="/wiki/Tasbih" title="Tasbih">phrases of adoration</a> such as repeating <a href="/wiki/Japa" title="Japa">chants</a> while performing other activities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvation">Salvation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">Salvation</a></div>
<p>Adherents of different religions generally disagree as to how to best <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> God and what is <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">God's plan</a> for mankind, if there is one. There are different approaches to reconciling the contradictory claims of monotheistic religions. One view is taken by exclusivists, who believe they are the <a href="/wiki/Chosen_people" title="Chosen people">chosen people</a> or have exclusive access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_truth" title="Absolute truth">absolute truth</a>, generally through <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> or encounter with the Divine, which adherents of other religions do not. Another view is <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">religious pluralism</a>. A pluralist typically believes that his religion is the right one, but does not deny the partial truth of other religions. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> preaches that <a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith)" title="Manifestation of God (Baháʼí Faith)">divine manifestations</a> include great prophets and teachers of many of the major religious traditions such as Krishna, Buddha, Jesus, Zoroaster, Muhammad, Bahá'ú'lláh and also preaches the unity of all religions and focuses on these multiple epiphanies as necessary for meeting the needs of humanity at different points in history and for different cultures, and as part of a scheme of <a href="/wiki/Progressive_revelation_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD)" title="Progressive revelation (Baháʼí)">progressive revelation</a> and education of humanity. An example of a pluralist view in Christianity is <a href="/wiki/Supersessionism" title="Supersessionism">supersessionism</a>, i.e., the belief that one's religion is the fulfillment of previous religions. A third approach is <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">relativistic inclusivism</a>, where everybody is seen as equally right; an example being <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a>: the doctrine that <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> is eventually available for everyone. A fourth approach is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncretic" title="Syncretic">syncretism</a>, mixing different elements from different religions. An example of syncretism is the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith">Faith</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a></div>
<p>As opposed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evidentialist" title="Evidentialist">evidentialist</a> position taken by those such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Swinburne" title="Richard Swinburne">Richard Swinburne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> in God is also sometimes held to be "<a href="/wiki/Reformed_epistemology" title="Reformed epistemology">properly basic</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Some theists agree that only some of the arguments for God's existence are compelling, but argue that faith is not a product of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, but requires risk. There would be no risk, they say, if the arguments for God's existence were as solid as the laws of logic, a position summed up by <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Pascal</a> as "the heart has reasons of which reason does not know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Inherent intuition about God is referred to in Islam as "<a href="/wiki/Fitra" title="Fitra">Fitra</a>", or “innate nature”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">Revelation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">Revelation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">Prophet</a></div>
<p>Revelation refers to some form of message communicated by God. This is usually proposed to occur through the use of <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> or <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Al-Maturidi</a> argued for the need for revelation because even though humans are intellectually capable of realizing God, human desire can divert the intellect and because <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghaib" title="Al-Ghaib">certain knowledge</a> cannot be known except for been specially given to prophets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason">Reason</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Athari" title="Athari">Traditionalist theology</a> holds that one should not opinionate beyond revelation to understand God's nature and frown upon rationalizations such as <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">speculative theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halverson-36_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halverson-36-95">[95]</a></sup> Notably, for <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic descriptions</a> such as the “Hand of God” and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God_in_Islam" title="Attributes of God in Islam">attributes of God</a>, they neither nullify such texts nor accept a literal hand but leave any ambiguity to God, called <a href="/wiki/Tafwid" title="Tafwid">tafwid</a>, without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bi-la_kaifa" title="Bi-la kaifa">asking how</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoover_2020_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover_2020-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halverson-3637_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halverson-3637-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_characteristics">Specific characteristics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Attributes of God in Christianity">Attributes of God in Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Attributes_of_Mercy" title="Thirteen Attributes of Mercy">Thirteen Attributes of Mercy</a></div>
<p>The characteristics attributed often differ according to the <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">conceptions of God</a> in the tradition from which they arise. Many traditions see God as eternal and incorporeal and  is seen as the perfect and constant embodiment of all virtues, powers and values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramsay_Custodians_of_Purity4_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsay_Custodians_of_Purity4-98">[98]</a></sup> Many characteristics are described in human terms and Christian theologian <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a> writes that one has to understand a "personal god" as an analogy. "To say that God is like a person is to affirm the divine ability and willingness to relate to others. This does not imply that God is human, or located at a specific point in the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles">Titles</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="425" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg/170px-Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>99 names of <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Sini_(script)" title="Sini (script)">Sini (script)</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">Names of God</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">Names of God in Islam</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> tradition, "the Bible has been the principal source of the conceptions of God". That the Bible "includes many different images, concepts, and ways of thinking about" God has resulted in perpetual "disagreements about how God is to be conceived and understood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Throughout the Hebrew and Christian Bibles there are many names for God. One of them is <i><a href="/wiki/Elohim" title="Elohim">Elohim</a></i>. Another one is <i><a href="/wiki/El_Shaddai" title="El Shaddai">El Shaddai</a></i>, translated "God Almighty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> A third notable name is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Elyon" title="El Elyon">El Elyon</a></i>, which means "The High God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Also noted in the Hebrew and Christian Bibles is the name "<a href="/wiki/I_Am_that_I_Am" title="I Am that I Am">I Am that I Am</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>God is described and referred in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> by certain <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">names or attributes</a>, the most common being <i><a href="/wiki/R-%E1%B8%A4-M" title="R-Ḥ-M">Al-Rahman</a></i>, meaning "Most Compassionate" and <i>Al-Rahim</i>, meaning "Most Merciful".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-104">[104]</a></sup> Many of these names are also used in the scriptures of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, a tradition in Hinduism, has a <a href="/wiki/List_of_titles_and_names_of_Krishna" title="List of titles and names of Krishna">list of titles and names of Krishna</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">Gender of God</a></div>
<p>The gender of God may be viewed as either a literal or an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> aspect of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> who, in classical western philosophy, transcends bodily form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">Polytheistic</a> religions commonly attribute to each of <i>the gods</i> a gender, allowing each to interact with any of the others, and perhaps with humans, sexually. In most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> religions, God has no counterpart with which to relate sexually. Thus, in classical western philosophy the <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> of this one-and-only deity is most likely to be an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analogical" title="Analogical">analogical</a> statement of how humans and God address, and relate to, each other. Namely, God is seen as begetter of the world and revelation which corresponds to the active (as opposed to the receptive) role in sexual intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biblical sources usually refer to God using male or paternal words and symbolism, except <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#1:26" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 1:26–27</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#123:2" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 123:2–3</a>, and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#15:8" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 15:8–10</a> (female); <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Hosea#11:3" title="s:Bible (King James)/Hosea">Hosea 11:3–4</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#32:18" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 32:18</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#66:13" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 66:13</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#49:15" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 49:15</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#42:14" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 42:14</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#131:2" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 131:2</a> (a mother); <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#32:11" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 32:11–12</a> (a mother eagle); and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#23:37" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 23:37</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#13:34" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 13:34</a> (a mother hen).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Image">Image</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">Incorporeality</a> and <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father_in_Western_art" title="God the Father in Western art">God the Father in Western art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="1574" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/220px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/330px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/440px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ahura Mazda (depiction is on the right, with high crown) presents <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a> (left) with the ring of kingship. (Relief at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a>, 3rd century CE)</div></div></div>
<p>In Zoroastrianism, during the early <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> was visually represented for worship. This practice ended during the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>. Zoroastrian <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a>, which can be traced to the end of the Parthian period and the beginning of the Sassanid, eventually put an end to the use of all images of Ahura Mazda in worship. However, Ahura Mazda continued to be symbolized by a dignified male figure, standing or on horseback, which is found in Sassanian investiture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983686_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983686-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Judaism, the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> often ascribes human features to God, however, many other passages describe God as formless and otherworldly. Judaism is <a href="/wiki/Aniconism" title="Aniconism">aniconic</a>, meaning it overly lacks material, physical representations of both the natural and supernatural worlds. Furthermore, the worship of idols is strictly forbidden. The traditional view, elaborated by figures such as <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, reckons that God is wholly incomprehensible and therefore impossible to envision, resulting in a historical tradition of "divine incorporeality". As such, attempting to describe God's "appearance" in practical terms is considered disrespectful to the deity and thus is deeply taboo, and arguably heretical.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a> cosmogony often depicts the creator god of the Old Testament as an evil lesser deity or <a href="/wiki/Demiurge#Gnosticism" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a>, while the higher benevolent god or <a href="/wiki/Monad_(Gnosticism)" title="Monad (Gnosticism)">Monad</a> is thought of as something beyond comprehension having immeasurable light and not in time or among things that exist, but rather is greater than them in a sense. All people are said to have a piece of God or <a href="/wiki/Divine_spark" title="Divine spark">divine spark</a> within them which has fallen from the immaterial world into the corrupt material world and is trapped unless <a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a> is attained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
