<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ascension,_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1628" data-file-width="1699" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg/170px-Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg/255px-Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg/340px-Ascension%2C_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ascension,_sacramentaire_de_Drogon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Use of the symbolic <a href="/wiki/Hand_of_God_(art)" title="Hand of God (art)">Hand of God</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Christ" title="Ascension of Christ">Ascension</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Drogo_Sacramentary" title="Drogo Sacramentary">Drogo Sacramentary</a>, c. 850</div></div></div>
<p>Early Christians believed that the words of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> 1:18: "No man has seen God at any time" and numerous other statements were meant to apply not only to God, but to all attempts at the depiction of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Cornwell_page_24_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_Cornwell_page_24-114">[114]</a></sup> However, later depictions of God are found. Some, like the <a href="/wiki/Hand_of_God_(art)" title="Hand of God (art)">Hand of God</a>, are depiction borrowed from Jewish art. Prior to the 10th century no attempt was made to use a human to symbolize <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art" title="Western art">Western art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Cornwell_page_24_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-James_Cornwell_page_24-114">[114]</a></sup> Yet, Western art eventually required some way to illustrate the presence of the Father, so through successive representations a set of artistic styles for symbolizing the Father using a man gradually emerged around the 10th century AD. A rationale for the use of a human is the belief that God created the soul of man in the image of his own (thus allowing human to transcend the other animals). It appears that when early artists designed to represent God the Father, fear and awe restrained them from a usage of the whole human figure. Typically only a small part would be used as the image, usually the hand, or sometimes the face, but rarely a whole human. In many images, the figure of the Son supplants the Father, so a smaller portion of the person of the Father is depicted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolphe_Napoléon_Didron_pages_169_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolphe_Napoléon_Didron_pages_169-115">[115]</a></sup> By the 12th century depictions of God the Father had started to appear in French <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>, which as a less public form could often be more adventurous in their iconography, and in <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> church windows in England. Initially the head or bust was usually shown in some form of frame of clouds in the top of the picture space, where the Hand of God had formerly appeared; the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">Baptism of Christ</a> on the famous <a href="/wiki/Baptismal_font_at_St_Bartholomew%27s_Church,_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Baptismal font at St Bartholomew's Church, Liège">baptismal font in Liège</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainer_of_Huy" title="Rainer of Huy">Rainer of Huy</a> is an example from 1118 (a Hand of God is used in another scene). Gradually the amount of the human symbol shown can increase to a half-length figure, then a full-length, usually enthroned, as in <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> of c. 1305 in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-116">[116]</a></sup> In the 14th century the <a href="/wiki/Naples_Bible" title="Naples Bible">Naples Bible</a> carried a depiction of God the Father in the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">Burning bush</a>. By the early 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_Duc_de_Berry" title="Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry">Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry</a> has a considerable number of symbols, including an elderly but tall and elegant full-length figure walking in the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>, which show a considerable diversity of apparent ages and dress. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battistero_di_San_Giovanni_(Florence)#Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Battistero di San Giovanni (Florence)">"Gates of Paradise" of the Florence Baptistry</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Lorenzo Ghiberti">Lorenzo Ghiberti</a>, begun in 1425 use a similar tall full-length symbol for the Father. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rohan_Book_of_Hours" title="Rohan Book of Hours">Rohan Book of Hours</a> of about 1430 also included depictions of God the Father in half-length human form, which were now becoming standard, and the Hand of God becoming rarer. At the same period other works, like the large Genesis <a href="/wiki/Altarpiece" title="Altarpiece">altarpiece</a> by the Hamburg painter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meister_Bertram" title="Meister Bertram">Meister Bertram</a>, continued to use the old depiction of Christ as <i><a href="/wiki/Logos_(Christianity)" title="Logos (Christianity)">Logos</a></i> in Genesis scenes. In the 15th century there was a brief fashion for depicting all three persons of the Trinity as <a href="/wiki/Trinity#Less_common_types_of_depiction" title="Trinity">similar or identical figures with the usual appearance of Christ</a>. In a Trinitarian <a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0" title="Pietà">Pietà</a>, God the Father is often symbolized using a man wearing a papal dress and a papal crown, supporting the dead Christ in his arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1667 the 43rd chapter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_Sobor_of_1666%E2%80%931667" title="Moscow Sobor of 1666–1667">Great Moscow Council</a> specifically included a ban on a number of symbolic depictions of God the Father and the Holy Spirit, which then also resulted in a whole range of other icons being placed on the forbidden list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> mostly affecting Western-style depictions which had been gaining ground in Orthodox icons. The Council also declared that the person of the Trinity who was the "Ancient of Days" was Christ, as <i>Logos</i>, not God the Father. However some icons continued to be produced in Russia, as well as <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, and other Orthodox countries.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_027_(6445021161).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1620" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg/170px-Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg/255px-Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg/340px-Istanbul_027_%286445021161%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_027_(6445021161).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Arabic script of "Allah" in the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Islam, Muslims believe that God (<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>) is beyond all comprehension and equal, and does not resemble any of his creations in any way. Thus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> are not <a href="/wiki/Iconodulism" title="Iconodulism">iconodules</a>, are not expected to visualize God, and instead of having pictures of Allah in their <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a>, typically have religious calligraphy written on the wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_approaches">Theological approaches</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Classical_theism" title="Classical theism">Classical theism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theistic_Personalism" title="Theistic Personalism">Theistic Personalism</a></div>
<p>Classical theists (such as ancient Greco-Medieval philosophers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christians" title="Eastern Orthodox Christians">Eastern Orthodox Christians</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[a]</a></sup> speak of God as a <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">divinely simple</a> '<a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">nothing</a>' that is completely <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a> (totally independent of all else), and having attributes such as <a href="/wiki/Immutability_(theology)" title="Immutability (theology)">immutability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impassibility" title="Impassibility">impassibility</a>, and timelessness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig98_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craig98-123">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">Theologians</a> of theistic personalism (the view held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rene_Descartes" title="Rene Descartes">Rene Descartes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Swinburne" title="Richard Swinburne">Richard Swinburne</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lane_Craig" title="William Lane Craig">William Lane Craig</a>, and most <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">modern evangelicals</a>) argue that God is most generally the ground of all being, immanent in and transcendent over the whole world of reality, with immanence and transcendence being the contrapletes of personality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> equated <a href="/wiki/Jungian_interpretation_of_religion" title="Jungian interpretation of religion">religious ideas of God</a> with transcendental metaphors of <a href="/wiki/Higher_consciousness" title="Higher consciousness">higher consciousness</a>, in which God can be just as easily be imagined "as an eternally flowing current of vital energy that endlessly changes shape ... as an eternally unmoved, unchangeable essence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jung_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-jung-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reconciling some of the attributes of God, particularly the attributes of the God of theistic personalism, generated important philosophical problems and debates. For example, God's omniscience may seem to imply that God knows how free agents will choose to act. If God does know this, their ostensible <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> might be illusory, or foreknowledge does not imply predestination, and if God does not know it, God may not be omniscient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wierenga_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wierenga-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Platinga-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Platinga_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Platinga_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Platinga, Alvin</a>. <li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The ulterior etymology is disputed. Apart from the unlikely hypothesis of adoption from a foreign tongue, the OTeut. "ghuba" implies as its preTeut-type either "*ghodho-m" or "*ghodto-m". The former does not appear to admit of explanation; but the latter would represent the neut. pple. of a root "gheu-". There are two Aryan roots of the required form ("*g,heu-" with palatal aspirate) one with meaning 'to invoke' (Skr. "hu") the other 'to pour, to offer sacrifice' (Skr "hu", Gr. χεηi;ν, OE "geotàn" Yete v). <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">OED Compact Edition, G, p. 267</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BARNHART323-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BARNHART323_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barnhart, Robert K. (1995). <i>The Barnhart Concise Dictionary of Etymology: the Origins of American English Words</i>, p. 323. <a href="/wiki/HarperCollins" title="HarperCollins">HarperCollins</a>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-06-270084-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-06-270084-7">0-06-270084-7</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/god" rel="nofollow">"<span class="cs1-kern-left"></span>'God' in Merriam-Webster (online)"</a>. Merriam-Webster, Inc<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 July</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%27God%27+in+Merriam-Webster+%28online%29&amp;rft.pub=Merriam-Webster%2C+Inc.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.merriam-webster.com%2Fdictionary%2Fgod&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_New_World_Dictionary" title="Webster's New World Dictionary">Webster's New World Dictionary</a>; "God n. ME &lt; OE, akin to Ger gott, Goth guth, prob. &lt; IE base * ĝhau-, to call out to, invoke &gt; Sans havaté, (he) calls upon; 1. any of various beings conceived of as supernatural, immortal, and having special powers over the lives and affairs of people and the course of nature; deity, esp. a male deity: typically considered objects of worship; 2. an image that is worshiped; idol 3. a person or thing deified or excessively honored and admired; 4. [G-] in monotheistic religions, the creator and ruler of the universe, regarded as eternal, infinite, all-powerful, and all-knowing; Supreme Being; the Almighty"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a class="external text" href="http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/God" rel="nofollow">Dictionary.com</a>; "God /gɒd/ noun: 1. the one Supreme Being, the creator and ruler of the universe. 2. the Supreme Being considered with reference to a particular attribute. 3. (lowercase) one of several deities, esp. a male deity, presiding over some portion of worldly affairs. 4. (often lowercase) a supreme being according to some particular conception: the God of mercy. 5. Christian Science. the Supreme Being, understood as Life, Truth, Love, Mind, Soul, Spirit, Principle. 6. (lowercase) an image of a deity; an idol. 7. (lowercase) any deified person or object. 8. (often lowercase) Gods, Theater. 8a. the upper balcony in a theater. 8b. the spectators in this part of the balcony."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://stanford.library.sydney.edu.au/archives/spr2008/entries/pantheism/" rel="nofollow">"Pantheism"</a>. <li id="cite_note-Dawe-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAlan_H._Dawe2011">Alan H. Dawe (2011). <i>The God Franchise: A Theory of Everything</i>. p. 48. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-473-20114-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-473-20114-2"><bdi>978-0-473-20114-2</bdi></a>. <q>Pandeism: This is the belief that God created the universe, is now one with it, and so, is no longer a separate conscious entity. This is a combination of pantheism (God is identical to the universe) and deism (God created the universe and then withdrew Himself).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+God+Franchise%3A+A+Theory+of+Everything&amp;rft.pages=48&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-473-20114-2&amp;rft.au=Alan+H.+Dawe&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSean_F._Johnston2009">Sean F. Johnston (2009). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historyofscience0000john/page/90" rel="nofollow"><i>The History of Science: A Beginner's Guide</i></a>. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historyofscience0000john/page/90" rel="nofollow">90</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-85168-681-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-85168-681-0"><bdi>978-1-85168-681-0</bdi></a>. <q>In its most abstract form, deism may not attempt to describe the characteristics of such a non-interventionist creator, or even that the universe is identical with God (a variant known as pandeism).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+History+of+Science%3A+A+Beginner%27s+Guide&amp;rft.pages=90&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-85168-681-0&amp;rft.au=Sean+F.+Johnston&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fhistoryofscience0000john%2Fpage%2F90&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPaul_Bradley2011">Paul Bradley (2011). <i>This Strange Eventful History: A Philosophy of Meaning</i>. p. 156. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-87586-876-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-87586-876-9"><bdi>978-0-87586-876-9</bdi></a>. <q>Pandeism combines the concepts of Deism and Pantheism with a god who creates the universe and then becomes it.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=This+Strange+Eventful+History%3A+A+Philosophy+of+Meaning&amp;rft.pages=156&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-87586-876-9&amp;rft.au=Paul+Bradley&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Fuller-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_45-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_45-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAllan_R._Fuller2010">Allan R. Fuller (2010). <i>Thought: The Only Reality</i>. p. 79. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-60844-590-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-60844-590-5"><bdi>978-1-60844-590-5</bdi></a>. <q>Pandeism is another belief that states that God is identical to the universe, but God no longer exists in a way where He can be contacted; therefore, this theory can only be proven to exist by reason. Pandeism views the entire universe as being from God and now the universe is the entirety of God, but the universe at some point in time will fold back into one single being which is God Himself that created all. Pandeism raises the question as to why would God create a universe and then abandon it? As this relates to pantheism, it raises the question of how did the universe come about what is its aim and purpose?</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Thought%3A+The+Only+Reality&amp;rft.pages=79&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-60844-590-5&amp;rft.au=Allan+R.+Fuller&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPeter_C._Rogers2009">Peter C. Rogers (2009). <i>Ultimate Truth, Book 1</i>. p. 121. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4389-7968-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4389-7968-7"><bdi>978-1-4389-7968-7</bdi></a>. <q>As with <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a> is derived from the Greek: 'pan'= all and 'theos' = God, it literally means "God is All" and "All is God." Pantheist purports that everything is part of an all-inclusive, indwelling, intangible God; or that the Universe, or nature, and God are the same. Further review helps to accentuate the idea that natural law, existence, and the Universe which is the sum total of all that is, was, and shall be, is represented in the theological principle of an abstract 'god' rather than an individual, creative Divine Being or Beings of any kind. This is the key element that distinguishes them from Panentheists and Pandeists. As such, although many religions may claim to hold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheistic" title="Pantheistic">Pantheistic</a> elements, they are more commonly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">Panentheistic</a> or Pandeistic in nature.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ultimate+Truth%2C+Book+1&amp;rft.pages=121&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4389-7968-7&amp;rft.au=Peter+C.+Rogers&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_56-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDawkins2006"><a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Dawkins, Richard</a> (2006). <i>The God Delusion</i>. <li id="cite_note-Dawkins-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dawkins_57-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFDawkins2006"><a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Dawkins, Richard</a> (23 October 2006). <a class="external text" href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/richard-dawkins/why-there-almost-certainl_b_32164.html" rel="nofollow">"Why There Almost Certainly Is No God"</a>. <i>The Huffington Post</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSagan1996"><a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Sagan, Carl</a> (1996). <i>The Demon Haunted World</i>. <li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFStephen_HawkingLeonard_Mlodinow2010">Stephen Hawking; Leonard Mlodinow (2010). <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/granddesign0000hawk" rel="nofollow"><i>The Grand Design</i></a></span>. Bantam Books. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/granddesign0000hawk/page/172" rel="nofollow">172</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-553-80537-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-553-80537-6"><bdi>978-0-553-80537-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Grand+Design&amp;rft.pages=172&amp;rft.pub=Bantam+Books&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-553-80537-6&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Hawking&amp;rft.au=Leonard+Mlodinow&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fgranddesign0000hawk&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Hepburn-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hepburn_60-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1" id="CITEREFHepburn2005">Hepburn, Ronald W. (2005) [1967]. "Agnosticism".  In Donald M. Borchert (ed.). <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Encyclopedia of Philosophy">The Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. Vol. 1 (2nd ed.). MacMillan Reference USA (Gale). p. 92. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-02-865780-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-02-865780-6"><bdi>978-0-02-865780-6</bdi></a>. <q>In the most general use of the term, agnosticism is the view that we do not know whether there is a God or not.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Agnosticism&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.pages=92&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=MacMillan+Reference+USA+%28Gale%29&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-02-865780-6&amp;rft.aulast=Hepburn&amp;rft.aufirst=Ronald+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span> (p. 56 in 1967 edition)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-RoweRoutledge-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-RoweRoutledge_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1" id="CITEREFRowe1998"><a href="/wiki/William_L._Rowe" title="William L. Rowe">Rowe, William L.</a> (1998). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VQ-GhVWTH84C&amp;q=agnosticism&amp;pg=PA122" rel="nofollow">"Agnosticism"</a>.  In Edward Craig (ed.). <i><a href="/wiki/Routledge_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. Taylor &amp; Francis. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-07310-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-07310-3"><bdi>978-0-415-07310-3</bdi></a>. <q>In the popular sense, an agnostic is someone who neither believes nor disbelieves in God, whereas an atheist disbelieves in God. In the strict sense, however, agnosticism is the view that human reason is incapable of providing sufficient rational grounds to justify either the belief that God exists or the belief that God does not exist. In so far as one holds that our beliefs are rational only if they are sufficiently supported by human reason, the person who accepts the philosophical position of agnosticism will hold that neither the belief that God exists nor the belief that God does not exist is rational.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Agnosticism&amp;rft.btitle=Routledge+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.pub=Taylor+%26+Francis&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-07310-3&amp;rft.aulast=Rowe&amp;rft.aufirst=William+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DVQ-GhVWTH84C%26q%3Dagnosticism%26pg%3DPA122&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1">"agnostic, agnosticism". <i>OED Online, 3rd ed</i>. Oxford University Press. 2012. <q><b>agnostic</b>. : <b>A</b>. n[oun]. :# A person who believes that nothing is known or can be known of immaterial things, especially of the existence or nature of God. :# In extended use: a person who is not persuaded by or committed to a particular point of view; a sceptic. Also: person of indeterminate ideology or conviction; an equivocator. : <b>B.</b> adj[ective]. :# Of or relating to the belief that the existence of anything beyond and behind material phenomena is unknown and (as far as can be judged) unknowable. Also: holding this belief. :# a. In extended use: not committed to or persuaded by a particular point of view; sceptical. Also: politically or ideologically unaligned; non-partisan, equivocal. <b>agnosticism</b> n. The doctrine or tenets of agnostics with regard to the existence of anything beyond and behind material phenomena or to knowledge of a First Cause or God.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=agnostic%2C+agnosticism&amp;rft.btitle=OED+Online%2C+3rd+ed.&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nielsen 2013: "Instead of saying that an atheist is someone who believes that it is false or probably false that there is a God, a more adequate characterization of atheism consists in the more complex claim that to be an atheist is to be someone who rejects belief in God for the following reasons ... : for an anthropomorphic God, the atheist rejects belief in God because it is false or probably false that there is a God; for a nonanthropomorphic God ... because the concept of such a God is either meaningless, unintelligible, contradictory, incomprehensible, or incoherent; for the God portrayed by some modern or contemporary theologians or philosophers ... because the concept of God in question is such that it merely masks an atheistic substance—e.g., "God" is just another name for love, or ... a symbolic term for moral ideals."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edwards 2005: "On our definition, an 'atheist' is a person who rejects belief in God, regardless of whether or not his reason for the rejection is the claim that 'God exists' expresses a false proposition. People frequently adopt an attitude of rejection toward a position for reasons other than that it is a false proposition. It is common among contemporary philosophers, and indeed it was not uncommon in earlier centuries, to reject positions on the ground that they are meaningless. Sometimes, too, a theory is rejected on such grounds as that it is sterile or redundant or capricious, and there are many other considerations which in certain contexts are generally agreed to constitute good grounds for rejecting an assertion."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rowe 1998: "As commonly understood, atheism is the position that affirms the nonexistence of God. So an atheist is someone who disbelieves in God, whereas a theist is someone who believes in God. Another meaning of 'atheism' is simply nonbelief in the existence of God, rather than positive belief in the nonexistence of God. ... an atheist, in the broader sense of the term, is someone who disbelieves in every form of deity, not just the God of traditional Western theology."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-boyer-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-boyer_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBoyer2001"><a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">Boyer, Pascal</a> (2001). <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/religionexplaine00boye" rel="nofollow"><i>Religion Explained</i></a></span>. New York: Basic Books. pp. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/religionexplaine00boye/page/142" rel="nofollow">142</a>–243. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-465-00696-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-465-00696-0"><bdi>978-0-465-00696-0</bdi></a>. <q>boyer modern soap opera.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Religion+Explained&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=142-243&amp;rft.pub=Basic+Books&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-465-00696-0&amp;rft.aulast=Boyer&amp;rft.aufirst=Pascal&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Freligionexplaine00boye&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ducasteljurgensen-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ducasteljurgensen_67-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFdu_CastelJurgensen,_Timothy_M.2008"><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Castel" title="Bertrand du Castel">du Castel, Bertrand</a>; Jurgensen, Timothy M. (2008). <i>Computer Theology</i>. <li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBarrett1996">Barrett, Justin (1996). <a class="external text" href="http://commonsenseatheism.com/wp-content/uploads/2009/09/Barrett-Conceptualizing-a-Nonnatural-Entity.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Conceptualizing a Nonnatural Entity: Anthropomorphism in God Concepts"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Cognitive Psychology</i>. <b>31</b> (3): 219–47. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1006%2Fcogp.1996.0017" rel="nofollow">10.1006/cogp.1996.0017</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/8975683" rel="nofollow">8975683</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:7646340" rel="nofollow">7646340</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Cognitive+Psychology&amp;rft.atitle=Conceptualizing+a+Nonnatural+Entity%3A+Anthropomorphism+in+God+Concepts&amp;rft.volume=31&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=219-47&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A7646340%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F8975683&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1006%2Fcogp.1996.0017&amp;rft.aulast=Barrett&amp;rft.aufirst=Justin&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcommonsenseatheism.com%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F2009%2F09%2FBarrett-Conceptualizing-a-Nonnatural-Entity.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-supernature-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-supernature_69-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRossano2007">Rossano, Matt (2007). <a class="external text" href="http://www2.selu.edu/Academics/Faculty/mrossano/recentpubs/Supernaturalizing.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Supernaturalizing Social Life: Religion and the Evolution of Human Cooperation"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Human Nature (Hawthorne, N.Y.)</i>. <b>18</b> (3): 272–94. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs12110-007-9002-4" rel="nofollow">10.1007/s12110-007-9002-4</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26181064" rel="nofollow">26181064</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:1585551" rel="nofollow">1585551</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 June</span> 2009</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Human+Nature+%28Hawthorne%2C+N.Y.%29&amp;rft.atitle=Supernaturalizing+Social+Life%3A+Religion+and+the+Evolution+of+Human+Cooperation&amp;rft.volume=18&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=272-94&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A1585551%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26181064&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2Fs12110-007-9002-4&amp;rft.aulast=Rossano&amp;rft.aufirst=Matt&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww2.selu.edu%2FAcademics%2FFaculty%2Fmrossano%2Frecentpubs%2FSupernaturalizing.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>, an English biologist, was the first to come up with the word <i>agnostic</i> in 1869 <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDixon2008">Dixon, Thomas (2008). <i>Science and Religion: A Very Short Introduction</i>. Oxford: Oxford University Press. p. 63. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7"><bdi>978-0-19-929551-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Science+and+Religion%3A+A+Very+Short+Introduction&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=63&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-929551-7&amp;rft.aulast=Dixon&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span> However, earlier authors and published works have promoted an agnostic points of view. They include <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">Protagoras</a>, a 5th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" title="Before Common Era">BCE</a> Greek philosopher. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">"The Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy – Protagoras (c. 490 – c. 420 BCE)"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081014181706/http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 14 October 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 October</span> 2008</span>. <q>While the pious might wish to look to the gods to provide absolute moral guidance in the relativistic universe of the Sophistic Enlightenment, that certainty also was cast into doubt by philosophic and sophistic thinkers, who pointed out the absurdity and immorality of the conventional epic accounts of the gods. Protagoras' prose treatise about the gods began 'Concerning the gods, I have no means of knowing whether they exist or not or of what sort they may be. Many things prevent knowledge including the obscurity of the subject and the brevity of human life.'</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Internet+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy+%E2%80%93+Protagoras+%28c.+490+%E2%80%93+c.+420+BCE%29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iep.utm.edu%2Fp%2Fprotagor.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod"></span></span>
</li>
